## Agent 6 / F — Singular anti-design cell-sum analogue

The current prompt asks whether the ordinary polylog-local \(Z_\alpha\)-amplitude obstruction and the deep singular anti-design obstruction can be put into a common finite-dimensional framework. They can, but the resulting prime-side problems live at different frequency ranges, so a single theorem would need to be scale-uniform and substantially stronger than either branch separately. 

---

# 1. Common finite-dimensional formulation

Both obstructions have the same abstract form.

Let \(\mathcal H_R\) be an \(R\)-dimensional test space, and let \(\mu\) be the relevant local packet distribution. The bad event is:

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\bigl(\langle \mu,\phi_j\rangle\bigr)_{j=1}^R
\right\|_{\ell^2}
\le Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The two cases differ only in what \(\mu\), \(\phi_j\), and the dual prime statistics are.

---

## Ordinary cell-sum branch

For

\[
F(t)=Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t)
=
\sum_n b_n e^{-itx_n}+\cdots,
\qquad
x_n=\lambda\log n,
\qquad
\lambda Q\asymp1,
\]

partition the packet-frequency nodes into cells \(I_r\), and define

\[
B_r
=
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}b_n.
\]

The obstruction is

\[
\boxed{
B_r\approx0
\quad\text{for all }r.
}
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\sum_r |B_r|^2\le Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is a finite-dimensional projection of the packet-frequency coefficient measure

\[
\mu_{\rm ord}
=
\sum_n b_n\delta_{x_n}
\]

onto cell indicators

\[
\phi_r(x)=1_{I_r}(x).
\]

A smoother basis version replaces \(1_{I_r}\) by localized bump functions or a stable orthonormal basis.

---

## Deep singular branch

For

\[
Q_{\rm sing}(x)
=
\sum_j w_jK_{\theta_j}(x)+Q_{\rm bg}(x),
\]

the obstruction is

\[
\boxed{
\sum_jw_j\sin(k\theta_j)+\mathcal B_k\approx0,
\qquad
3\le k\le R+3.
}
\]

This is the projection of the singular packet measure

\[
\mu_{\rm sing}
=
\sum_j w_j\delta_{\theta_j}
\]

onto the trigonometric basis

\[
\phi_k(\theta)=\sin(k\theta),
\qquad 3\le k\le R+3,
\]

with shifted target \(-\mathcal B_k\).

Thus the deep singular obstruction is also a finite-dimensional moment/cell-sum cancellation:

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\bigl(\langle\mu_{\rm sing},\sin(k\theta)\rangle+\mathcal B_k\bigr)_{k=3}^{R+3}
\right\|_{\ell^2}
\le Q^{-A}.
}
\]

---

# 2. Can singular sine moments be rewritten as cell sums?

Yes, after changing basis.

The sine-moment vector

\[
M_k=\sum_jw_j\sin(k\theta_j)
\]

can be transformed into localized angular cell sums by a stable trigonometric reconstruction if one uses \(R\) modes and \(O(R)\) angular cells.

Let \(\Theta_s\) be cells in \(\theta\)-space and define

\[
C_s=\sum_{\theta_j\in \Theta_s}w_j.
\]

Then, up to conditioning losses,

\[
(M_k)_{3\le k\le R+3}
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
(C_s)_s
\]

provided:

1. the \(\theta_j\) lie in a compact region away from endpoint singularities;
2. the trigonometric basis is normalized stably;
3. one accepts losses at most \(\exp(O(R\log R))=Q^{O(1)}\).

So the singular anti-design may be rewritten as:

\[
\boxed{
C_s+\mathcal C_s^{\rm bg}\approx0
\quad\text{for all angular cells }s.
}
\]

This is formally analogous to the ordinary obstruction

\[
B_r\approx0
\quad\text{for all packet-frequency cells }r.
\]

---

# 3. Prime-side comparison

The analogy becomes weaker after applying the explicit formula.

---

## Ordinary branch prime vector

Ordinary cell sums use packet-frequency nodes

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n,
\qquad
\lambda Q\asymp1.
\]

The active prime range is

\[
\boxed{
\log n\asymp Q.
}
\]

The cell-sum vector is schematically

\[
B_r(m_0)
=
\sum_{\lambda\log n\in I_r}
z_n e^{-im_0\log n}.
\]

So the ordinary anti-cancellation theorem is a nonrecurrence statement for a fixed packet-frequency prime vector at conductor scale:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_r
\left|
\sum_{\lambda\log n\in I_r}z_n e^{-im_0\log n}
\right|^2
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This lives at the natural AFE scale.

---

## Singular branch prime vector

Singular sine moments require test functions \(h_k\) satisfying

\[
\langle K_\theta,h_k\rangle\approx\sin(k\theta).
\]

By the explicit formula, the prime statistic is

\[
\mathcal P_k(\gamma,c)
=
2\Re
\sum_n
\frac{\Lambda(n)}{\sqrt n}
n^{-i\gamma}
\widehat h_k(c\log n).
\]

To recover modes through \(k\le R+3\), the probes need bandwidth at least polynomial in \(R\):

\[
\operatorname{bandwidth}(h_k)\gtrsim R^C.
\]

Therefore the prime range is

\[
\boxed{
\log n\lesssim \frac{R^C}{c}.
}
\]

In the deep singular regime,

\[
cQ\ll(\log Q)^{-C_0},
\]

so

\[
\frac1c\gg Q(\log Q)^{C_0}.
\]

Thus

\[
\boxed{
\log n\lesssim \frac{R^C}{c}
\gg Q(\log Q)^{C_0}.
}
\]

This is generally much longer than the ordinary conductor-scale packet

\[
\log n\asymp Q.
\]

---

# 4. Candidate unified theorem

A formally unified theorem would be:

## Scale-uniform actual-prime anti-design theorem

Let \(L\) be a frequency scale and let \(\Psi_1,\ldots,\Psi_R\) be a stable test family with Fourier support in

\[
|\xi|\lesssim L.
\]

Define prime-vector statistics

\[
\mathcal P_j(m;s)
=
2\Re
\sum_n
\frac{\Lambda(n)}{\sqrt n}
n^{-im}
\widehat\Psi_j(s\log n),
\]

where \(s\) is the local scale:

\[
s=\lambda
\quad\text{in the ordinary branch,}
\]

or

\[
s=c
\quad\text{in the singular branch.}
\]

Then actual zeta should satisfy a lower bound

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\mathbf P_R(m;s)-\mathbf T_R(m;s)
\right\|_2
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

unless the corresponding canonical source curvature already has Gate 1 mass.

In this form:

- ordinary branch: \(sQ\asymp1\), \(L\asymp1\), prime range \(\log n\asymp Q\);
- singular branch: \(sQ\ll1\), \(L\asymp R^C\), prime range \(\log n\lesssim R^C/s\).

This theorem would cover both ordinary cell sums and singular sine moments.

---

# 5. Why the unified theorem is probably too strong

Although the algebraic form is unified, the analytic arithmetic is not.

The ordinary theorem asks for nonrecurrence at natural conductor scale:

\[
\log n\asymp Q.
\]

The singular theorem asks for nonrecurrence over a much longer prime range:

\[
\log n\lesssim R^C/c.
\]

Since \(c^{-1}\gg Q(\log Q)^{C_0}\), this range can exceed the ordinary AFE/conductor range by polylogarithmic or worse factors.

Therefore a unified theorem would need to control prime-vector anti-concentration uniformly across scales

\[
\log n\lesssim \frac{L}{s},
\]

including scales much larger than \(Q\).

That is strictly stronger than the ordinary Wronskian arithmetic input.

---

# 6. Conclusion: same framework, separate arithmetic inputs

The two branches share a common finite-dimensional structure:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite packet-mode projections cannot all be small.}
}
\]

But their explicit-formula prime realizations differ:

| Branch | Local obstruction | Test basis | Prime range |
|---|---|---|---|
| Ordinary | cell sums \(B_r\approx0\) | packet-frequency cells / bumps | \(\log n\asymp Q\) |
| Deep singular | sine moments \(\sum_jw_j\sin(k\theta_j)+\mathcal B_k\approx0\) | derivative / trigonometric probes | \(\log n\lesssim R^C/c\) |

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Ordinary and singular obstructions are formally unified but arithmetically distinct.}
}
\]

A single scale-uniform actual-prime nonrecurrence theorem could cover both, but it would be stronger than what is needed for the ordinary route and would need long-prime control.

So the practical recommendation is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Treat ordinary and singular branches as separate arithmetic inputs.}
}
\]

Ordinary branch target:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_r
\left|
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}z_n e^{-im_0\log n}
\right|^2
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Deep singular branch target:

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\mathbf P_R(\gamma,c)-\mathbf G_R(\gamma,c)-\mathbf T_R(\gamma,c)
\right\|_2
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

They are the same kind of anti-design/nonrecurrence statement, but at different prime scales.

_Thought for 6s_

## Agent 6 / F — Ordinary/singular anti-design separation

The ordinary and deep singular branches share the same **finite-dimensional anti-design form**, but their prime-side realizations occur at different scales. The clean conclusion is: they should remain separate theorem targets unless one is willing to prove a much stronger scale-uniform actual-prime nonrecurrence theorem. The latest brief explicitly warns that the ordinary and singular branches have different prime ranges, \(\log n\asymp Q\) versus \(\log n\lesssim R^C/c\), and should be treated as separate arithmetic inputs. 

---

# 1. Common finite-dimensional anti-design framework

Both obstructions can be written as

\[
\boxed{
\langle \mu,\phi_j\rangle\approx 0,
\qquad
1\le j\le J,
}
\]

where \(J\asymp R\), and \(R\asymp \log Q/\log\log Q\).

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\bigl(\langle \mu,\phi_j\rangle\bigr)_{j=1}^{J}
\right\|_{\ell^2}
\le Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The difference is the measure \(\mu\), the test basis \(\phi_j\), and the prime-side transform.

---

# 2. Ordinary branch

The ordinary obstruction is

\[
B_r(m_0)=
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}
\approx0
\]

for all packet-frequency cells \(I_r\).

Here

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n,
\qquad
\lambda Q\asymp1,
\qquad
\log n\asymp Q.
\]

Define the discrete packet-frequency measure

\[
\mu_{\rm ord}
=
\sum_n z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}\delta_{x_n}.
\]

Then

\[
B_r=\langle \mu_{\rm ord},1_{I_r}\rangle.
\]

So the ordinary anti-design event is

\[
\boxed{
\sum_r|\langle \mu_{\rm ord},1_{I_r}\rangle|^2\le Q^{-A}.
}
\]

A smoother version replaces \(1_{I_r}\) by a stable localized basis \(\phi_r\), but the finite-dimensional structure is the same.

The corresponding prime-side theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_r
\left|
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}
\right|^2
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is a conductor-scale, packet-frequency nonrecurrence theorem.

Prime range:

\[
\boxed{
\log n\asymp Q.
}
\]

---

# 3. Deep singular branch

The deep singular obstruction is

\[
\sum_jw_j\sin(k\theta_j)+\mathcal B_k\approx0,
\qquad
3\le k\le R+3.
\]

Define the angular packet measure

\[
\mu_{\rm sing}
=
\sum_jw_j\delta_{\theta_j}.
\]

Then

\[
\sum_jw_j\sin(k\theta_j)
=
\langle \mu_{\rm sing},\sin(k\theta)\rangle.
\]

So the singular anti-design event is

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\bigl(
\langle \mu_{\rm sing},\sin(k\theta)\rangle+\mathcal B_k
\bigr)_{k=3}^{R+3}
\right\|_{\ell^2}
\le Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is formally the same structure as ordinary cell-sum cancellation, but in a trigonometric moment basis.

Using probes \(h_k\) satisfying

\[
\langle K_\theta,h_k\rangle\approx \sin(k\theta),
\]

the explicit formula produces prime statistics

\[
\mathcal P_k(\gamma,c)
=
2\Re
\sum_n
\frac{\Lambda(n)}{\sqrt n}
n^{-i\gamma}
\widehat h_k(c\log n).
\]

Thus the singular theorem target is

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\mathbf P_R(\gamma,c)
-
\mathbf G_R(\gamma,c)
-
\mathbf T_R(\gamma,c)
\right\|_2
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Prime range:

\[
\boxed{
\log n\lesssim R^C/c.
}
\]

Since this is the deep singular regime,

\[
cQ\ll(\log Q)^{-C_0},
\]

so

\[
1/c\gg Q(\log Q)^{C_0},
\]

and therefore

\[
\boxed{
R^C/c\gg Q(\log Q)^{C_0}
}
\]

up to powers of \(R\). This usually exceeds the ordinary conductor-scale range.

---

# 4. Can one theorem cover both?

Yes, formally.

A unified theorem would look like this:

## Scale-uniform actual-prime anti-design theorem

Let \(s>0\) be a local scale and let \(\Psi_1,\ldots,\Psi_J\) be a stable test family. Define

\[
\mathcal P_j(m;s)
=
2\Re
\sum_n
\frac{\Lambda(n)}{\sqrt n}
n^{-im}
\widehat\Psi_j(s\log n).
\]

Then for every admissible canonical packet,

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\mathbf P(m;s)-\mathbf T(m;s)
\right\|_2
\ge Q^{-A},
}
\]

unless Gate 1 already holds by direct mass/dominance.

Specializations:

Ordinary:

\[
s=\lambda,\qquad \lambda Q\asymp1,
\qquad \operatorname{supp}\widehat\Psi_j\asymp1,
\qquad \log n\asymp Q.
\]

Singular:

\[
s=c,\qquad cQ\ll(\log Q)^{-C},
\qquad \operatorname{supp}\widehat\Psi_j\lesssim R^C,
\qquad \log n\lesssim R^C/c.
\]

This would cover both.

But it is too strong for the current proof architecture: the singular version requires long-prime control beyond the ordinary packet-frequency scale.

---

# 5. Why the branches should remain separate

The ordinary theorem is a natural-scale statement:

\[
\boxed{
\log n\asymp Q.
}
\]

The singular theorem is a long-prime statement:

\[
\boxed{
\log n\lesssim R^C/c,
\qquad c^{-1}\gg Q(\log Q)^{C_0}.
}
\]

Therefore the singular theorem asks for anti-concentration of a prime vector over a much longer range. This is not a minor strengthening of the ordinary theorem; it is a different arithmetic input.

The ordinary branch also has a direct AFE/packet-frequency interpretation, while the singular branch arises from regularized derivative probes and an uncertainty principle: detecting \(R\) local sine moments forces bandwidth at least polynomial in \(R\), hence long-prime support.

So a single unified theorem is possible only if it controls all local scales \(s\), including deep singular \(s=c\). That is substantially stronger than needed for the ordinary Wronskian route.

---

# 6. Final theorem targets

## Ordinary theorem target

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_r
\left|
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}
\right|^2
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This supports polylog-local \(Z_\alpha\)-amplitude and hence

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Prime range:

\[
\boxed{\log n\asymp Q.}
\]

## Singular theorem target

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\mathbf P_R(\gamma,c)
-
\mathbf G_R(\gamma,c)
-
\mathbf T_R(\gamma,c)
\right\|_2
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This excludes

\[
\sum_jw_j\sin(k\theta_j)+\mathcal B_k\approx0,
\qquad
3\le k\le R+3,
\]

and closes the deep singular branch via dominance.

Prime range:

\[
\boxed{\log n\lesssim R^C/c.}
\]

---

# 7. Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The ordinary and singular obstructions share one anti-design template.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{They do not share the same arithmetic scale.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Treat them as separate theorem targets.}
}
\]

A unified scale-uniform actual-prime nonrecurrence theorem would be elegant, but it would need long-prime control and is strictly stronger than the ordinary route requires.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 06:46:13)

_Thought for 5s_

## Agent 6 / F — Connect cell-sum theorem to remaining Wronskian incidence stack

The multiblock cell-sum theorem is now the cleanest path to the first Wronskian factor,

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

The latest coordinator brief states that the numerical fork is clean enough to park, and that the next theorem target is the multiblock cell-sum non-cancellation input. 

---

# 1. Exact implication chain

Let

\[
F(t)=Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t),
\qquad
F(0)=0,
\qquad
\lambda Q\asymp1.
\]

Use multiblock packet-frequency support

\[
\mathcal B_K
=
\left\{
n:cQ\le \log n\le cQ+\frac{KQ}{R}
\right\},
\qquad K\ge 8.
\]

Define cell sums

\[
B_r(m_0)
=
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n},
\qquad
x_n=\lambda\log n.
\]

In the canonical main+dual version,

\[
B_r
=
B_r^+
+
e^{i\Theta_Z(m_0,\alpha)}B_r^-
+
B_r^{\rm bg}
+
B_r^E.
\]

The desired arithmetic input is

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
\sum_r |B_r(m_0)|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Then the analytic chain is:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_r |B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\boxed{
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2\,dt\ge Q^{-A'}
}
\]

for a safe radius such as

\[
a\asymp R
\quad\text{or}\quad
a\asymp 2R,
\]

with \(K\ge8\) ensuring the support spans multiple natural cells rather than collapsing to the already-cancelled scalar condition \(F(0)=0\).

Then

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2\,dt\ge Q^{-A'}
\Longrightarrow
\sup_{|t|\le a}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-A''}.
\]

Finally, by the already-settled amplitude-to-derivative lemma, together with outer analytic control on a larger disk,

\[
\boxed{
\sup_{|t|\le a}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-A''}
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A'''}.
}
\]

Thus the full black-box implication is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{multiblock cell-sum non-cancellation}
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

---

# 2. Where this fits in the Wronskian route

The ordinary Wronskian is

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)
=
\det(C_\ell(\mathcal D)G_j(m,\alpha))_{0\le j,\ell\le R+1},
\]

with

\[
G_0=Z_\alpha,
\qquad
G_{j+1}=\mathcal D^j q''_{\can},
\quad 0\le j\le R.
\]

At a zero

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,
\]

expansion along the \(Z_\alpha\)-row gives

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)
=
\langle \mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha),\mathbf M(m_0)\rangle.
\]

So after proving the multiblock cell-sum theorem, we get only the first factor:

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The Wronskian route still needs:

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A},
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
|\langle \mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha),\mathbf M(m_0)\rangle|
\ge
Q^{-A}
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\,|\mathbf M(m_0)|.
}
\]

Equivalently, it still needs

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

---

# 3. Does the cell-sum theorem help with \(\mathbf M\)?

Only weakly.

The cell-sum theorem is about the \(Z_\alpha\)-row:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
\text{\(Z_\alpha\)-packet coefficients do not cancel cellwise}.
\]

The minor vector \(\mathbf M\), however, is built from the \(q''_{\can}\)-jet rows. Its expansion has subset-product frequencies:

\[
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_{|S|=R+1}
\mathbf A_S e^{-im\log N_S}
+
\text{dual/background/error}.
\]

Thus \(\mathbf M\) lives in a different frequency algebra from the first-row cell sums. The cell theorem does not directly imply

\[
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

The right remaining theorem for \(\mathbf M\) is still a zero-set large sieve or zero-set nonconcentration statement:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
}
\]

A pointwise consequence would be

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

outside a controlled exceptional set, or uniformly if one can rule out concentration of \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros inside the small-\(\mathbf M\) locus.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum theorem does not replace the \(\mathbf M\) theorem.}
}
\]

It only strengthens the first Wronskian row.

---

# 4. Does the cell-sum theorem give angle/nonalignment?

No, not by itself.

The cell-sum theorem proves that

\[
\mathbf z'\neq0
\]

with polynomial lower bound. It says nothing about the direction of \(\mathbf z'\) relative to the cofactor vector \(\mathbf M\).

The remaining angle theorem is

\[
\boxed{
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge
Q^{-A}
|\mathbf z'|\,|\mathbf M|.
}
\]

This is not a consequence of lower bounds for \(|\mathbf z'|\) and \(|\mathbf M|\). Phase-relaxed models can have

\[
|\mathbf z'|\gg Q^{-A},
\qquad
|\mathbf M|\gg Q^{-A},
\qquad
\mathbf z'\perp\mathbf M.
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{angle/nonalignment remains the full arithmetic incidence problem.}
}
\]

The cell-sum theorem removes one possible way the Wronskian could vanish, namely \(\mathbf z'\) being too small. It does not rule out cancellation in the pairing.

---

# 5. Difficulty comparison

## 5.1 Multiblock cell-sum non-cancellation

Target:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_r
\left|
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}
\right|^2
\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This is the smallest current arithmetic input. It is a conductor-scale prime-log nonrecurrence theorem. The scalar zero condition only imposes

\[
\sum_r B_r\approx0,
\]

so the theorem says actual zeta cannot arrange

\[
B_r\approx0
\quad\text{for all cells }r.
\]

Relative difficulty:

\[
\boxed{
\text{hard, but the most tractable remaining ordinary-route subclaim.}
}
\]

## 5.2 Zero-set large sieve for \(\mathbf M\)

Target:

\[
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}.
\]

This is harder because \(\mathbf M\) has subset-product frequencies

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S,
\]

not just packet-frequency cell sums. The sampling occurs on the zero set of \(Z_\alpha\), so one needs control of kernels

\[
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
=
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)}.
\]

Relative difficulty:

\[
\boxed{
\text{harder than cell-sum; likely a genuine zero-set spectral large sieve.}
}
\]

## 5.3 Direct Wronskian incidence / angle theorem

Target:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Or

\[
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge
Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
\]

This is the hardest ordinary-scale theorem. It includes the possibility that both factors are large but aligned almost orthogonally.

Relative difficulty:

\[
\boxed{
\text{hardest; full actual-zeta incidence/nonalignment theorem.}
}
\]

---

# 6. Roadmap after proving the cell-sum theorem

If the multiblock cell-sum theorem is proved, the next exact theorem to attack is:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The most promising formulation is not pointwise first, but averaged over zeros:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\ Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
}
\]

This should be attacked through the zero-sampling kernel

\[
K_J(\xi)=
\sum_{m_j:Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi}.
\]

Only after a usable lower bound for \(\mathbf M\) is available should agents focus entirely on the angle/incidence theorem

\[
\boxed{
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge
Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
}
\]

That final step likely requires the deepest actual-zeta arithmetic.

---

# 7. Recommended next agent allocation

After the cell-sum theorem is either proved or fully reduced, use the agents as follows:

1. **Two agents on zero-set large sieve for \(\mathbf M\).**  
   Focus on \(K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)\), argument-principle sampling, and subset-product resonance control.

2. **Two agents on direct incidence / angle theorem.**  
   Search for actual-zeta nonalignment, folded main/dual arithmetic, and possible resultant-type separation.

3. **One agent on canonical transfer and error-budget audit.**  
   Ensure \(\mathbf M\), \(\mathbf z'\), and the Wronskian are still the full canonical \(B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2\) object.

4. **One agent on singular branch.**  
   Keep deep singular anti-design separate: it requires long-prime actual-vector nonrecurrence, not ordinary Wronskian algebra.

---

# Bottom line

The multiblock cell-sum theorem is valuable because it would close:

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

But it does **not** close Gate 1.

After proving it, the ordinary Wronskian route still needs:

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
}
\]

So the next exact theorem after cell-sum non-cancellation should be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{zero-set large sieve for }\mathbf M.
}
\]

## Agent 6 / F — Post-cell-sum roadmap to Gate 1

The latest coordinator brief fixes the current ordinary-route focus: prove the fixed-width multiblock cell-sum theorem first, because that closes the \(\mathbf z'\) factor. It also states that this does **not** close Gate 1; the next exact theorem is still a lower bound for the cofactor vector \(\mathbf M\), likely via a zero-set large sieve. 

---

# 1. What the multiblock cell-sum theorem gives

Assume the theorem:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
\sum_r |B_r(m_0)|^2\ge Q^{-A},
\]

where

\[
B_r
=
B_r^+
+
e^{i\Theta_Z(m_0,\alpha)}B_r^-
+
B_r^{\rm bg}
+
B_r^E.
\]

Together with the fixed-width cell-sum analytic package, this gives:

\[
\sum_r |B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}
\Longrightarrow
\int_{-a}^{a}|Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t)|^2\,dt\ge Q^{-A'}
\]

for \(a\asymp R\) or \(2R\), hence

\[
\sup_{|t|\le a}|Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t)|\ge Q^{-A''}.
\]

Then the already-settled amplitude-to-derivative lemma gives

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A'''}.
}
\]

So the cell-sum theorem proves exactly the first Wronskian factor:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum non-cancellation}
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

---

# 2. What remains afterward

At a zero

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,
\]

the basis-normalized Wronskian expands as

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)
=
\langle\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha),\mathbf M(m_0)\rangle.
\]

After the cell-sum theorem, we know only:

\[
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

We still need:

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A},
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge
Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
}
\]

Equivalently, the ordinary route still requires:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Thus, proving the cell-sum theorem closes only one failure mode: the possibility that the \(Z_\alpha\)-row is too flat. It does not prove cofactor nonvanishing, and it does not prove nonalignment.

---

# 3. Next exact theorem: lower bound for \(\mathbf M\)

The next theorem should be:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Here \(\mathbf M\) is the cofactor/minor vector built from the \(q''_{\can}\)-jet rows. Its schematic expansion is

\[
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_{|S|=R+1}
\mathbf A_S e^{-im\Omega_S}
+
\text{dual/background/error},
\]

where

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S.
\]

This is fundamentally different from the \(Z_\alpha\)-cell theorem. The cell theorem concerns first-row packet-frequency coefficients. The \(\mathbf M\) theorem concerns subset-product frequencies from exterior powers of the \(q''_{\can}\)-jet block.

---

# 4. Zero-set large sieve formulation

The natural route is an averaged theorem over \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\ Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
}
\]

Equivalently, \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros should not concentrate inside the small-\(\mathbf M\) locus.

Substituting the expansion of \(\mathbf M\), the sampling sum becomes

\[
\sum_{m_j}w_J(m_j)|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
=
\sum_{S,T}
\langle\mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
+
\text{dual/background/error},
\]

where

\[
K_J(\xi)
=
\sum_{m_j:Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi}.
\]

So the zero-set large sieve is a spectral estimate for

\[
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
\]

at subset-product frequency differences.

A useful theorem would have the form:

\[
\boxed{
K_J(0)\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2
-
\left|
\sum_{S\ne T}
\langle\mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
\right|
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
}
\]

The diagonal term is supported by exterior coefficient mass. The hard part is controlling off-diagonal subset-product resonances sampled on the \(Z_\alpha\)-zero set.

---

# 5. Does the cell-sum theorem help \(\mathbf M\)?

Only indirectly.

It may provide:

\[
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A},
\]

and possibly some local nonflatness information about \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros. But it does **not** directly constrain \(\mathbf M\), because \(\mathbf M\) is made from \(q''_{\can}\)-jet rows, not from the \(Z_\alpha\)-row.

Specifically:

\[
\text{cell sums use frequencies } \lambda\log n,
\]

while

\[
\mathbf M\text{ uses subset-product frequencies } \Omega_S=\log N_S.
\]

These are related only through the same underlying prime-log orbit, not through a direct linear implication.

So the cell-sum theorem does not replace the zero-set large sieve. It only makes the first row quantitatively usable.

---

# 6. Does the cell-sum theorem help the angle?

No direct implication.

Even after

\[
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

and even after a hypothetical

\[
|\mathbf M|\ge Q^{-A},
\]

one can still have

\[
\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle\approx0.
\]

The angle theorem remains:

\[
\boxed{
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge
Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
}
\]

This is essentially the direct incidence theorem in cofactor form.

The cell-sum theorem rules out a small \(\mathbf z'\). It does not control the direction of \(\mathbf z'\) relative to \(\mathbf M\).

---

# 7. Recommended next allocation after cell-sum

After the multiblock cell-sum theorem is proved or reduced as far as possible:

## Two agents on \(\mathbf M\) zero-set large sieve

Focus:

\[
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
=
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)}.
\]

Tasks:

- derive the argument-principle expression for \(K_J\);
- isolate diagonal and off-diagonal terms;
- classify subset-product resonances;
- prove or refute a usable zero-set spectral large sieve.

## Two agents on direct angle/incidence

Focus:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Tasks:

- search for actual-zeta nonalignment;
- revisit folded main/dual arithmetic only if it gives a concrete invariant;
- test whether a resultant or common-small-value theorem can yield polynomial separation.

## One agent on canonical audit

Ensure all objects remain canonical:

\[
q''_{\can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2,
\]

and that high derivatives through the Wronskian order remain inside the accepted \(Q^{O(1)}\) error budget.

## One agent on singular branch

Keep the deep singular branch separate. Its theorem target remains:

\[
\left\|
\mathbf P_R-\mathbf G_R-\mathbf T_R
\right\|_2
\ge Q^{-A},
\]

or equivalently actual-zeta exclusion of finite sine-moment anti-design.

---

# 8. Final roadmap

The ordinary Gate 1 path is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{multiblock cell-sum theorem}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\]

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow\quad\text{combine with}
\]

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf M|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow\quad\text{combine with}
\]

\[
\boxed{
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge
Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\]

\[
\boxed{
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite-jet anti-design}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Gate 1.}
}
\]

The immediate next theorem after the cell-sum theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{zero-set large sieve for }\mathbf M.
}
\]

The final theorem is still the angle/incidence nonalignment.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 06:51:37)

_Thought for 4s_

## Agent 6 / F — Roadmap after cell-sum theorem

The current target is the fixed-width multiblock cell-sum theorem. If proved, it closes only the first Wronskian factor, namely \(|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}\). The next theorem remains a lower bound for the cofactor vector \(\mathbf M\), followed by the angle/incidence theorem. 

---

# 1. Completed implication from cell sums

Assume the fixed-width multiblock cell theorem:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
\sum_r |B_r(m_0)|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Here

\[
B_r
=
B_r^+
+
e^{i\Theta_Z}B_r^-
+
B_r^{\rm bg}
+
B_r^E.
\]

The analytic package gives:

\[
\sum_r |B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}
\Longrightarrow
\sup_{|t|\le R}|Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t)|\ge Q^{-A'}.
\]

The amplitude-to-derivative lemma then gives:

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A''}.
}
\]

Thus the cell-sum theorem completes the first Wronskian input:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum theorem}
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

It does not by itself prove Gate 1.

---

# 2. Next theorem: lower bound for \(\mathbf M\)

At a zero

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,
\]

the Wronskian expands as

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)
=
\langle \mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha),\mathbf M(m_0)\rangle.
\]

After proving \(|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}\), the next exact theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Here \(\mathbf M\) is the cofactor vector of the \(q''_{\can}\)-jet block. It is not a degree-one cell-sum object. It is an exterior-power object with subset-product frequencies.

A schematic expansion is:

\[
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_{|S|=R+1}
\mathbf A_S e^{-im\Omega_S}
+
\text{dual/background/error},
\]

where

\[
\boxed{
\Omega_S=\log N_S.
}
\]

If \(S=\{n_1,\ldots,n_{R+1}\}\), then

\[
N_S=n_1n_2\cdots n_{R+1},
\qquad
\Omega_S=\sum_{n\in S}\log n.
\]

---

# 3. Zero-set large sieve formulation

The natural route is an averaged theorem over zeros of \(Z_\alpha\):

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\ Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
}
\]

Substitute the expansion of \(\mathbf M\):

\[
\sum_{m_j}w_J(m_j)|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
=
\sum_{S,T}
\langle \mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
+
\text{dual/background/error},
\]

where the zero-sampling kernel is

\[
K_J(\xi)
=
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\ Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi}.
\]

So the theorem reduces to controlling

\[
\boxed{
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
}
\]

for subset-product frequency differences.

The diagonal contribution is

\[
K_J(0)\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2,
\]

and should be positive if exterior coefficient mass is already known. The difficulty is the off-diagonal sum:

\[
\sum_{S\ne T}
\langle \mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T).
\]

The theorem needed is a zero-set spectral large sieve saying that these off-diagonal terms cannot cancel the diagonal by more than a polynomial factor.

---

# 4. Degree-one cell theorem versus degree-\((R+1)\) \(\mathbf M\) theorem

The cell theorem is degree-one in the AFE frequencies:

\[
B_r(m_0)
=
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n e^{-im_0\log n}.
\]

Its frequencies are

\[
\log n.
\]

The \(\mathbf M\) theorem is degree-\((R+1)\):

\[
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_{|S|=R+1}
\mathbf A_S e^{-im\log N_S}.
\]

Its frequencies are

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S=\sum_{n\in S}\log n.
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum non-cancellation does not imply the \(\mathbf M\) lower bound.}
}
\]

It proves that the \(Z_\alpha\)-row is not flat, but it does not control the exterior minors of the \(q''_{\can}\)-jet block.

The \(\mathbf M\) theorem is harder because:

1. the number of subset frequencies is much larger;
2. subset-product resonances can occur;
3. sampling is restricted to the zero set of \(Z_\alpha\);
4. main/dual/background/error terms must remain canonical;
5. the required estimate is vector-valued, not scalar.

---

# 5. Final angle/incidence theorem remains after \(\mathbf M\)

Even if we prove

\[
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

and

\[
|\mathbf M|\ge Q^{-A},
\]

we still need

\[
\boxed{
|\langle \mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge
Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
}
\]

This is the true nonalignment/incidence theorem.

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The cell theorem removes the possibility that \(\mathbf z'\) is too small. The \(\mathbf M\) theorem removes the possibility that the cofactor vector is too small. The angle theorem removes the remaining possibility that both are large but nearly orthogonal.

---

# 6. Recommended next six-agent allocation after cell theorem

Once the cell-sum theorem is proved or reduced as far as possible, use the next round as follows.

## Agents 1–2: zero-set large sieve for \(\mathbf M\)

Two agents should attack

\[
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}.
\]

Agent 1 should focus on the sampling kernel

\[
K_J(\xi)
=
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi},
\]

possibly via the argument principle.

Agent 2 should focus on subset-product resonance control:

\[
\Omega_S-\Omega_T=\log(N_S/N_T).
\]

The goal is to separate diagonal mass from off-diagonal resonance.

## Agents 3–4: angle/incidence theorem

Two agents should attack

\[
|\langle \mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
\]

Agent 3 should look for direct actual-orbit incidence or resultant-style separation.

Agent 4 should revisit folded main/dual arithmetic only if it produces a concrete nonalignment invariant. Otherwise, it should explicitly reduce the issue to subset-product nonrecurrence.

## Agent 5: canonical audit and numerical standby

Agent 5 should not run broad tests. It should maintain the canonical audit trail:

\[
q''_{\can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2,
\]

verify that any \(\mathbf M\) theorem uses the same canonical object, and prepare targeted stress tests only if theorem agents propose a new failure mode.

## Agent 6: singular branch

One agent should keep the deep singular branch separate. Its target remains the long-prime anti-design theorem:

\[
\left\|
\mathbf P_R-\mathbf G_R-\mathbf T_R
\right\|_2
\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This is not part of the ordinary Wronskian cell-sum proof and should not be mixed into the \(\mathbf M\) zero-set large sieve.

---

# 7. Bottom line

If the fixed-width multiblock cell-sum theorem is proved, the ordinary Gate 1 route advances to:

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}
\quad\text{secured.}
}
\]

The next theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The preferred formulation is the zero-set large sieve:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\ Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
}
\]

After that, the final ordinary-route blocker is the angle/incidence theorem:

\[
\boxed{
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
}
\]

That is the remaining path to proving the Wronskian lower bound, then finite-jet anti-design, then Gate 1.

## Agent 6 / F — Post-cell theorem transition to \(\mathbf M\)

Assume the fixed-width multiblock cell theorem has been proved:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This closes only the first Wronskian factor. The next ordinary Gate 1 theorem is the cofactor-vector lower bound for \(\mathbf M\), followed by the angle/incidence theorem. The current coordinator brief explicitly frames the next transition this way: after cell-sum, attack \(\mathbf M\), then angle/incidence, while keeping the singular branch separate. 

---

# 1. Wronskian status after the cell theorem

At a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero,

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,
\]

the basis-normalized Wronskian expands as

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)
=
\langle \mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha),\mathbf M(m_0)\rangle.
\]

The cell theorem gives

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Therefore the remaining failure modes are exactly:

\[
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ll Q^{-A},
\]

or

\[
\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)\perp \mathbf M(m_0)
\quad\text{to polynomial accuracy}.
\]

So after the cell theorem, the ordinary Wronskian route still needs:

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge
Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
}
\]

---

# 2. Cofactor-vector theorem

The next exact theorem should be:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Here \(\mathbf M\) is the cofactor vector of the \(q''_{\can}\)-jet block. Schematically,

\[
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_{|S|=R+1}
\mathbf A_S e^{-im\Omega_S}
+
\text{dual/background/error}.
\]

The relevant frequencies are subset-product frequencies:

\[
\boxed{
\Omega_S=\log N_S,\qquad |S|=R+1.
}
\]

If

\[
S=\{n_1,\ldots,n_{R+1}\},
\]

then

\[
N_S=n_1\cdots n_{R+1},
\qquad
\Omega_S=\sum_{n\in S}\log n.
\]

This is a higher-degree object than the cell theorem. The cell theorem involved degree-one frequencies \(\log n\); \(\mathbf M\) involves exterior powers and subset products.

---

# 3. Zero-set large sieve formulation

The natural averaged theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\ Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
}
\]

Substitute the subset-product expansion:

\[
\sum_{m_j}w_J(m_j)|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
=
\sum_{S,T}
\langle \mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
+
\text{dual/background/error},
\]

where

\[
\boxed{
K_J(\xi)
=
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\ Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi}.
}
\]

The desired large sieve is a bound showing that the diagonal contribution dominates the off-diagonal contribution:

\[
K_J(0)\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2
\not\approx
-
\sum_{S\ne T}
\langle \mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T).
\]

So the core analytic object is the zero-sampling kernel

\[
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
\]

on subset-product frequency differences.

---

# 4. Comparison with the degree-one cell large sieve

The cell theorem is degree one:

\[
B_r(m_0)
=
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n e^{-im_0\log n}.
\]

Its frequencies are

\[
\log n.
\]

The \(\mathbf M\) theorem is degree \(R+1\):

\[
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_{|S|=R+1}
\mathbf A_S e^{-im\log N_S}.
\]

Its frequencies are

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S.
\]

Therefore the \(\mathbf M\) large sieve is strictly harder:

1. many more frequencies;
2. subset-product resonances;
3. vector-valued coefficients;
4. zero-set sampling rather than pointwise cell sums;
5. full canonical \(q''_{\can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2\) bookkeeping.

The degree-one cell theorem does **not** imply the \(\mathbf M\) theorem. It only proves that the \(Z_\alpha\)-row is not flat.

---

# 5. Angle/incidence remains after \(\mathbf M\)

Even if the zero-set large sieve proves

\[
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A},
\]

the final ordinary obstruction remains:

\[
\boxed{
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
}
\]

This is equivalent to the actual Wronskian incidence theorem at \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is not a norm lower bound. It is a nonalignment theorem. It will likely require actual-zeta arithmetic beyond diagonal large-sieve estimates.

---

# 6. Next six-agent allocation after the cell theorem

## Agents 1–2: \(\mathbf M\) zero-set large sieve

Assign two agents to:

\[
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}.
\]

One should focus on the zero-sampling kernel

\[
K_J(\xi)
=
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi},
\]

possibly via the argument principle. The other should focus on subset-product resonance control:

\[
\Omega_S-\Omega_T=\log(N_S/N_T).
\]

## Agents 3–4: angle/incidence theorem

Assign two agents to:

\[
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
\]

One should search for direct actual-orbit incidence or resultant-type separation. The other should revisit folded main/dual arithmetic only if it yields a concrete nonalignment invariant.

## Agent 5: canonical audit

Assign one agent to canonical transfer/error audit. Every \(\mathbf M\) and Wronskian theorem must use

\[
q''_{\can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2
\]

with high-derivative \(E_2\) control through the required Wronskian order.

## Agent 6: singular branch

Keep one agent on the deep singular branch separately. Its target remains:

\[
\left\|
\mathbf P_R-\mathbf G_R-\mathbf T_R
\right\|_2
\ge Q^{-A},
\]

or equivalently actual-zeta exclusion of singular sine-moment anti-design.

---

# 7. Roadmap summary

After proving the cell theorem, the ordinary route is:

\[
\text{cell theorem}
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Then prove:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Then prove:

\[
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge
Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
\]

Then obtain:

\[
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Then finite-jet anti-design follows, and then Gate 1.

So the next exact theorem after the cell-sum theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{zero-set large sieve for }\mathbf M.
}
\]

## Agent 6 / F — Exact roadmap after the cell theorem

Assume the cell theorem is proved:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

The current prompt asks for the next theorem stage. The active architecture is: first prove the cell theorem to secure the \(Z_\alpha\)-row derivative vector, then move to the cofactor vector \(\mathbf M\), then to the angle/incidence theorem. 

---

# 1. Wronskian expansion at a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero

The basis-normalized Wronskian is

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)
=
\det(C_\ell(\mathcal D)G_j(m,\alpha))_{0\le j,\ell\le R+1}.
\]

At

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,
\]

expansion along the \(Z_\alpha\)-row gives

\[
\boxed{
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)
=
\langle \mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha),\mathbf M(m_0)\rangle.
}
\]

Here

\[
\mathbf z'
=
\bigl((-1)^\ell C_\ell(\mathcal D)Z_\alpha(m_0)\bigr)_{\ell=1}^{R+1},
\]

and \(\mathbf M\) is the cofactor/minor vector determined by the \(q''_{\can}\)-jet block.

After the cell theorem, the first factor is controlled:

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

---

# 2. Next theorem: cofactor-vector lower bound

The next theorem is

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This theorem is independent of the cell theorem in substance. The cell theorem controls the \(Z_\alpha\)-row. The vector \(\mathbf M\) is built from the \(q''_{\can}\)-jet rows.

Schematic expansion:

\[
\boxed{
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_{|S|=R+1}
\mathbf A_S e^{-im\Omega_S}
+
\text{dual/background/error},
}
\]

with

\[
\boxed{
\Omega_S=\log N_S.
}
\]

For

\[
S=\{n_1,\ldots,n_{R+1}\},
\]

\[
N_S=n_1\cdots n_{R+1},
\qquad
\Omega_S=\sum_{n\in S}\log n.
\]

So \(\mathbf M\) is a degree-\((R+1)\) exterior-product object, not a degree-one cell-sum object.

---

# 3. Zero-set large sieve for \(\mathbf M\)

The preferred formulation is an averaged zero-set large sieve:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\ Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
}
\]

Substitute the expansion of \(\mathbf M\):

\[
\sum_{m_j}w_J(m_j)|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
=
\sum_{S,T}
\langle \mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
+
\text{dual/background/error}.
\]

The zero-sampling kernel is

\[
\boxed{
K_J(\xi)
=
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi}.
}
\]

The diagonal term is

\[
K_J(0)\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2.
\]

The problem is to show that off-diagonal terms do not cancel this:

\[
\sum_{S\ne T}
\langle \mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
\]

must be controlled at polynomial loss.

---

# 4. Degree-one cell sieve versus degree-\((R+1)\) \(\mathbf M\) sieve

The cell theorem is degree one:

\[
B_r(m)
=
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n e^{-im\log n}.
\]

Its frequencies are

\[
\log n.
\]

The \(\mathbf M\) theorem is degree \(R+1\):

\[
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_{|S|=R+1}
\mathbf A_S e^{-im\log N_S}.
\]

Its frequencies are

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S.
\]

Thus the \(\mathbf M\) large sieve is much harder:

\[
\boxed{
\text{degree-one cell nonrecurrence}
\not\Rightarrow
\text{degree-\((R+1)\) cofactor nonrecurrence}.
}
\]

The main new difficulties are:

1. subset-product frequency collisions and near-collisions;
2. large exterior coefficient families;
3. vector-valued coefficients \(\mathbf A_S\);
4. zero-set sampling rather than pointwise cell evaluation;
5. canonical main/dual/background/error terms in \(q''_{\can}\).

The cell theorem should be viewed as a successful first-row theorem, not as a substitute for the cofactor theorem.

---

# 5. What remains after \(\mathbf M\)

Even after proving

\[
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A},
\qquad
|\mathbf M|\ge Q^{-A},
\]

the Wronskian lower bound still needs the angle theorem:

\[
\boxed{
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge
Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
}
\]

Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is the actual incidence/nonalignment theorem. It is strictly stronger than size lower bounds.

---

# 6. Next six-agent allocation after the cell theorem

## Agents 1–2: \(\mathbf M\) zero-set large sieve

Assign two agents to the cofactor-vector theorem.

**Agent 1:** zero-sampling kernel and argument principle.

Target:

\[
K_J(\xi)
=
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi}.
\]

Tasks:

- express \(K_J\) via an argument-principle contour integral;
- estimate \(K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)\);
- determine the usable interval length \(J\);
- quantify diagonal versus off-diagonal behavior.

**Agent 2:** subset-product resonance control.

Target:

\[
\Omega_S-\Omega_T=\log(N_S/N_T).
\]

Tasks:

- classify exact collisions \(N_S=N_T\);
- bound near-collisions;
- estimate multiplicities of subset-product frequencies;
- determine when off-diagonal terms can be absorbed.

## Agents 3–4: angle/incidence

Assign two agents to the final Wronskian incidence theorem.

**Agent 3:** direct actual-orbit incidence.

Target:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Tasks:

- formulate a common-small-value theorem;
- test resultant or exponential-polynomial separation;
- identify what actual-zeta arithmetic would enforce nonalignment.

**Agent 4:** folded main/dual nonalignment.

Target:

\[
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
\]

Tasks:

- decompose pure-main, pure-dual, and mixed contributions;
- isolate possible commutator/skew invariants;
- split \(|\alpha|Q\gtrsim \log Q\) versus \(|\alpha|Q\ll1\);
- reduce unresolved cases to explicit folded subset-product nonrecurrence.

## Agent 5: canonical audit

Assign one agent to check every \(\mathbf M\) theorem against the canonical object

\[
q''_{\can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2.
\]

Tasks:

- verify \(B_2,D_2,E_2\) are included;
- check high-derivative \(E_2\) through Wronskian order;
- ensure finite truncations transfer to the canonical object;
- audit ordinary versus singular scale boundaries.

## Agent 6: singular branch

Keep one agent on the deep singular branch.

Target:

\[
\left\|
\mathbf P_R-\mathbf G_R-\mathbf T_R
\right\|_2
\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Tasks:

- continue explicit-formula singular anti-design analysis;
- track long-prime range \(\log n\lesssim R^C/c\);
- keep this branch separate from ordinary \(\mathbf M\) large sieve work.

---

# 7. Roadmap summary

The ordinary route after the cell theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell theorem}
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

then

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

preferably via

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}.
}
\]

then

\[
\boxed{
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|
}
\]

then

\[
\boxed{
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

then finite-jet anti-design, and finally Gate 1.

The immediate next theorem after cell-sum is therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{zero-set large sieve for }\mathbf M.
}

## Assistant (2026-04-27 07:02:21)

## Agent 6 / F — Stay on cell theorem or move to \(\mathbf M\)?

Recommendation:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Stay on the cell theorem for one more focused round.}
}
\]

Reason: the cell theorem is still the smallest ordinary-route arithmetic unlock. It is degree-one in the prime-log phases, empirically clean, analytically connected to \(|\mathbf z'|\), and structurally simpler than the \(\mathbf M\) theorem. The \(\mathbf M\) theorem is degree \(R+1\), subset-product valued, zero-set sampled, and strictly harder. The current prompt also identifies the cell theorem’s two unresolved pieces precisely: same-branch off-diagonal control and main/dual cell-vector nonalignment, especially in the hard \(|\alpha|Q\ll1\) branch. 

---

# 1. Exact unresolved cell-theorem inputs

The target is

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
|B(m_0)|_2^2
=
\sum_r |B_r(m_0)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

With

\[
B=B^+ + e^{i\Theta_Z}B^- + B^{\rm bg}+B^E,
\]

the proof has reduced to two main inputs.

---

## Input 1 — Same-branch off-diagonal nonrecurrence

Need:

\[
\boxed{
|B^+|_2^2+|B^-|_2^2\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

For the plus branch,

\[
|B^+|_2^2=D^+ + O^+,
\]

where

\[
D^+=\sum_n |z_n^+|^2
\]

and

\[
O^+
=
\sum_r
\sum_{\substack{n\ne n'\\x_n,x_{n'}\in I_r}}
z_n^+\overline{z_{n'}^+}
e^{-im_0\log(n/n')}.
\]

The needed theorem is

\[
O^+ + O^-
\ge
-(1-Q^{-A})(D^+ + D^-).
\]

Equivalently: actual prime-log phases should not make every same-branch cell vanish simultaneously.

This is a fixed-\(m_0\), same-cell off-diagonal nonrecurrence theorem.

---

## Input 2 — Main/dual cell-vector nonalignment

Need:

\[
\boxed{
|B^+ + e^{i\Theta_Z}B^-|_2
\ge
Q^{-A}(|B^+|_2+|B^-|_2).
}
\]

The dangerous case is

\[
B^+\approx -e^{i\Theta_Z}B^-.
\]

Equivalently, one must control

\[
\langle B^+,B^-\rangle.
\]

The easy branch is probably

\[
|\alpha|Q\gtrsim \log Q,
\]

where branch dominance may help. The hard branch remains

\[
\boxed{
|\alpha|Q\ll1.
}
\]

There the needed theorem is a folded main/dual actual-orbit nonrecurrence statement, using the complementary product relation

\[
nn'\approx \frac{m_0}{2\pi}
\]

inside matched cells.

---

## Optional fallback — projected cell mass

If raw cell mass is too strong, use

\[
\boxed{
|\Pi_{\le R+1}B|_2^2\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is closer to jet nonflatness than raw cell mass. But it should remain a fallback: raw cell mass gives local \(L^2\)-amplitude directly, while projected mass adds another conditioning layer.

---

# 2. Are these easier than \(\mathbf M\)?

Yes.

The cell theorem is degree one. Its frequencies are

\[
\log n.
\]

The cofactor theorem is degree \(R+1\). It has frequencies

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S,
\qquad |S|=R+1,
\]

where

\[
N_S=\prod_{n\in S}n.
\]

Thus \(\mathbf M\) involves subset-product frequencies

\[
\Omega_S=\sum_{n\in S}\log n.
\]

That introduces:

\[
\text{subset-product resonances,}
\]

\[
\text{many more frequencies,}
\]

\[
\text{vector-valued exterior coefficients,}
\]

\[
\text{zero-set sampling on } Z_\alpha(m)=0,
\]

and full canonical \(q''_{\can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2\) bookkeeping.

By contrast, the cell theorem asks whether the actual degree-one prime-log orbit can force

\[
B_r\approx0
\quad\forall r
\]

under only the scalar condition

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell theorem} < \mathbf M\text{ theorem in arithmetic complexity.}
}
\]

---

# 3. Why proving the cell theorem first remains correct

The ordinary Wronskian route at a zero is

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)
=
\langle \mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha),\mathbf M(m_0)\rangle.
\]

The cell theorem gives

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Without this, the Wronskian can fail trivially because the first row is too flat. So the cell theorem removes the most accessible failure mode before attacking the harder cofactor and angle problems.

The theorem stack is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell theorem}
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

then

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0\Rightarrow |\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

then

\[
\boxed{
|\langle \mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|.
}
\]

This ordering is still correct.

---

# 4. Next two subtheorems to prove

Stay on the cell theorem and split the next work into exactly these two subtheorems.

---

## Subtheorem A — Same-branch cell mass

Prove:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
|B^+|_2^2+|B^-|_2^2\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

A useful formulation is:

\[
\boxed{
O^+ + O^-
\ge
-(1-Q^{-A})(D^+ + D^-).
}
\]

This should be attacked as a fixed-\(m_0\), same-cell off-diagonal nonrecurrence theorem.

---

## Subtheorem B — Main/dual cell nonalignment

Prove:

\[
\boxed{
|B^+ + e^{i\Theta_Z}B^-|_2
\ge
Q^{-A}(|B^+|_2+|B^-|_2).
}
\]

Split into:

\[
|\alpha|Q\gtrsim\log Q
\]

where branch dominance may close the case, and

\[
|\alpha|Q\ll1
\]

where the true folded main/dual nonrecurrence theorem is needed.

---

# 5. Recommended next six-agent allocation

\[

}

   Prove or reduce \(O^+\ge-(1-Q^{-A})D^+\).

4. **Agent 4 — Easy tilted branch \(|\alpha|Q\gtrsim\log Q\).**  
   Try to close via branch dominance.

5. **Agent 5 — Projected-cell fallback.**  
   Formalize \(|\Pi_{\le R+1}B|_2\) as backup and determine if it weakens the arithmetic burden.

6. **Agent 6 — Synthesis/audit.**  
   Combine the outputs into the exact theorem stack and decide whether the cell theorem is closed, reduced to one named nonrecurrence input, or should be paused in favor of \(\mathbf M\).

## Agent 6 / F — Synthesis: close, name, or pause the cell theorem

The cell theorem is **not closed yet**, but it has now been reduced to a crisp actual-orbit nonrecurrence package. I would keep the cell theorem active for **one more round**, but shift from further decomposition to proving or naming the exact nonrecurrence theorem. The latest prompt correctly identifies the two remaining inputs: same-branch off-diagonal nonrecurrence and main/dual cell-vector nonalignment. 

---

# 1. The two exact remaining subtheorems

## Subtheorem A — Same-branch off-diagonal nonrecurrence

We need

\[
\boxed{
O^+ + O^-
\ge
-(1-Q^{-A})(D^+ + D^-).
}
\]

Here

\[
|B^+|_2^2=D^+ + O^+,
\qquad
|B^-|_2^2=D^- + O^-,
\]

with

\[
D^\pm=\sum_n |z_n^\pm|^2,
\]

and

\[
O^+
=
\sum_r
\sum_{\substack{n\ne n'\\x_n,x_{n'}\in I_r}}
z_n^+\overline{z_{n'}^+}
e^{-im_0\log(n/n')},
\]

\[
O^-
=
\sum_r
\sum_{\substack{n\ne n'\\x_n^-,x_{n'}^-\in I_r}}
z_n^-\overline{z_{n'}^-}
e^{+im_0\log(n/n')}.
\]

Equivalent formulation:

\[
\boxed{
|B^+|_2^2+|B^-|_2^2\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This rules out simultaneous cellwise self-cancellation in both branches.

---

## Subtheorem B — Main/dual cell-vector nonalignment

We need

\[
\boxed{
|B^+ + e^{i\Theta_Z}B^-|_2
\ge
Q^{-A}(|B^+|_2+|B^-|_2).
}
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
|\langle B^+,B^-\rangle|
\le
(1-Q^{-A})|B^+|_2|B^-|_2.
}
\]

The hard case is the near-symmetric branch

\[
|\alpha|Q\ll1.
\]

The useful structural relation there is the folded same-cell condition

\[
nn'\approx \frac{m_0}{2\pi},
\]

with residual phase

\[
e^{-im_0\log(nn'/(m_0/2\pi))}.
\]

That is the exact bilinear phase object to attack.

---

# 2. What has been proved by regime?

## Regime 1: \(|\alpha|Q\gtrsim\log Q\)

This regime looks **closeable modulo same-branch mass**.

Reason: the main/dual coefficient ratio has exponential tilt of the form

\[
e^{\pm \alpha Q},
\]

up to polynomial factors from smoothing, gamma terms, log weights, and cell normalization. With

\[
|\alpha|Q\ge C\log Q
\]

for \(C\) large enough, one branch should dominate by a fixed polynomial power of \(Q\):

\[
|B^{\rm dom}|_2\ge Q^c |B^{\rm sub}|_2.
\]

Then

\[
|B^+ + e^{i\Theta_Z}B^-|_2
\ge
|B^{\rm dom}|_2-|B^{\rm sub}|_2
\ge
Q^{-O(1)}(|B^+|_2+|B^-|_2),
\]

assuming the dominant branch itself has not cell-collapsed. So this branch reduces to Subtheorem A.

Classification:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Tilted regime: conditionally closeable once same-branch mass is proved.}
}
\]

---

## Regime 2: \(|\alpha|Q\ll\log Q\)

The tilt no longer guarantees dominance. The main/dual nonalignment theorem remains active.

Classification:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Intermediate regime: unresolved; likely same folded nonrecurrence as hard branch, with weaker asymmetry.}
}
\]

---

## Regime 3: \(|\alpha|Q\ll1\)

This is the core hard case.

Same-branch mass still needs actual-orbit nonrecurrence. Main/dual nonalignment becomes a folded bilinear nonrecurrence theorem:

\[
\boxed{
|\langle B^+,B^-\rangle|
\le
(1-Q^{-A})|B^+|_2|B^-|_2.
}
\]

Generic phase-relaxed models can saturate Cauchy–Schwarz, so the actual one-parameter orbit must be used.

Classification:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Hard symmetric regime: unresolved; exact folded actual-orbit nonrecurrence needed.}
}
\]

---

# 3. Is the cell theorem closed?

No.

It is not closed because neither of the following is yet a theorem:

\[
O^+ + O^-
\ge
-(1-Q^{-A})(D^+ + D^-),
\]

\[
|B^+ + e^{i\Theta_Z}B^-|_2
\ge Q^{-A}(|B^+|_2+|B^-|_2)
\quad
\text{for }|\alpha|Q\ll1.
\]

But it is also no longer usefully decomposable at the local-algebra level. The remaining assertions are both actual-orbit nonrecurrence inputs.

---

# 4. Named theorem statement

I would name the remaining package:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Fixed-Width Cell Nonrecurrence Theorem.}
}
\]

## Theorem target

Let

\[
\lambda Q\asymp1,
\qquad
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\]

and let

\[
\mathcal B_{\rm wide}
=
\{n:c_-Q\le \log n\le c_+Q\},
\qquad
0<c_-<c_+<1/2.
\]

Let \(I_r\) be cells of width \(\asymp R^{-1}\), and define the canonical cell vector

\[
B_r
=
B_r^+
+
e^{i\Theta_Z}B_r^-
+
B_r^{\rm bg}
+
B_r^E.
\]

Then for every admissible ordinary packet center satisfying

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,
\]

one has

\[
\boxed{
\sum_r|B_r(m_0)|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Equivalently, in split form:

\[
\boxed{
|B^+|_2^2+|B^-|_2^2\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
|B^+ + e^{i\Theta_Z}B^-|_2
\ge
Q^{-A}(|B^+|_2+|B^-|_2).
}
\]

This theorem implies

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A'}
\]

via the already-settled analytic cell-sum-to-amplitude-to-derivative chain.

---

# 5. Should we stay on the cell theorem?

Yes, but only for one more focused proof attempt.

The next round should no longer ask agents to “explore the route.” It should ask them to prove or refute the named theorem above.

If that round only restates that the theorem requires actual-orbit nonrecurrence, then the cell theorem should be parked as a named input and agents should move to \(\mathbf M\).

---

Allocation:

   \]

   Prove or refute:

   \le(1-Q^{-A})|B^+|_2|B^-|_2

   in \(|\alpha|Q\ll1\).

   Formulate the full cell theorem as avoidance of a fixed cell-flat variety, with recurrence-cost and Diophantine requirements explicit.

5. **Agent 5 — Projected fallback.**  
   Determine whether the named theorem should be raw cell mass or projected cell mass.

6. **Agent 6 — Decision/audit.**  
   After the round, decide one of:
   \[
   \text{proved},\quad
   \text{named input},\quad
   \text{countermechanism},\quad
   \text{move to }\mathbf M.
   \]

## Agent 6 / F — Synthesis and stop condition for cell theorem

The cell theorem is now reduced as far as the current decomposition usefully takes it. I recommend **naming the remaining input** as a single arithmetic theorem, then moving the next major agent allocation mostly to \(\mathbf M\), while keeping one agent on the hard symmetric cell branch. The latest coordinator brief says the previous upload only sharpened the plus branch into actual prime-log cell-flat avoidance, without closing it, and asks this round to decide whether to keep decomposing or name the theorem. 

---

# 1. Current unresolved subclaims

The target is

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
|B(m_0)|_2^2
=
\sum_r|B_r(m_0)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

with

\[
B=B^+ + e^{i\Theta_Z}B^-+B^{\rm bg}+B^E.
\]

The remaining unresolved subclaims are exactly:

\[
\boxed{
O^+\ge -(1-Q^{-A})D^+.
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
O^+ + O^-
\ge
-(1-Q^{-A})(D^+ + D^-).
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
|B^+ + e^{i\Theta_Z}B^-|_2
\ge
Q^{-A}(|B^+|_2+|B^-|_2).
}
\]

The first is plus-branch cell-flat avoidance. The second is combined same-branch mass. The third is folded main/dual nonalignment.

---

# 2. Regime status

## Tilted branch: \(|\alpha|Q\gtrsim\log Q\)

This regime is conditionally closeable.

The branch coefficient ratio has the expected form

\[
\frac{|z^-|}{|z^+|}
=
Q^{O(1)}e^{\alpha Q},
\]

or the inverse depending on the sign of \(\alpha\). Once

\[
|\alpha|Q\ge C\log Q
\]

with \(C\) large enough to dominate the \(Q^{O(1)}\) losses, one branch should dominate. Then

\[
|B^+ + e^{i\Theta_Z}B^-|_2
\ge
|B^{\rm dom}|_2-|B^{\rm sub}|_2
\]

closes the main/dual nonalignment **modulo same-branch mass** and canonical error absorption.

Status:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Tilted branch: reduced to same-branch mass.}
}
\]

## Hard symmetric branch: \(|\alpha|Q\ll1\)

This remains open.

The dangerous condition is

\[
B^+\approx -e^{i\Theta_Z}B^-.
\]

The cross term is controlled by

\[
\langle B^+,B^-\rangle
=
\sum_r
\sum_{\substack{x_n^+\in I_r\\x_{n'}^-\in I_r}}
z_n^+\overline{z_{n'}^-}
e^{-im_0\log(nn')}.
\]

The folded same-cell relation gives

\[
nn'\approx \frac{m_0}{2\pi},
\]

so the hard theorem is a folded bilinear nonrecurrence statement preventing Cauchy–Schwarz saturation.

Status:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Hard symmetric branch: unresolved actual-orbit folded bilinear nonrecurrence.}
}
\]

---

# 3. The named theorem

The remaining package should now be named.

## Fixed-Width Multiblock Cell Nonrecurrence Theorem

Let

\[
\lambda Q\asymp1,
\qquad
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\]

and let

\[
\mathcal B_{\rm wide}
=
\{n:c_-Q\le \log n\le c_+Q\},
\qquad
0<c_-<c_+<\frac12.
\]

Partition

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n
\]

into cells

\[
I_r,\qquad |I_r|\asymp R^{-1},
\qquad M_{\rm cell}\asymp R.
\]

Define

\[
B_r^+
=
\sum_{x_n^+\in I_r}
z_n^+(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n},
\]

\[
B_r^-
=
\sum_{x_n^-\in I_r}
z_n^-(\alpha,m_0)e^{+im_0\log n},
\]

and

\[
B_r=
B_r^+
+
e^{i\Theta_Z(m_0,\alpha)}B_r^-
+
B_r^{\rm bg}
+
B_r^E.
\]

Then for every admissible ordinary-scale center satisfying

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,
\]

one has

\[
\boxed{
\sum_r|B_r(m_0)|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Equivalently, the theorem asserts the actual one-parameter prime-log orbit avoids the cell-flat variety

\[
P_{\rm cell}v=0
\]

to polynomial accuracy at \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros.

Split form:

\[
\boxed{
|B^+|_2^2+|B^-|_2^2\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
|B^+ + e^{i\Theta_Z}B^-|_2
\ge Q^{-A}(|B^+|_2+|B^-|_2).
}
\]

This theorem implies

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A'}
\]

by the already-settled cell-mass \(\Rightarrow\) local amplitude \(\Rightarrow\) derivative-vector chain.

---

# 4. Can another cell-theorem round decompose it further?

Only marginally.

The useful decomposition is already exhausted:

1. same-branch self-cancellation;
2. main/dual nonalignment;
3. tilted versus symmetric \(\alpha\)-regimes;
4. raw versus projected cell mass fallback.

Further local algebra will likely restate the same need:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual prime-log orbit nonrecurrence.}
}
\]

The theorem is false in phase-relaxed models, so no purely finite-dimensional or local-functional argument will close it. It must use the one-parameter structure

\[
e^{-im_0\log n}.
\]

Thus another full round solely on cell algebra is unlikely to generate new structure unless it attacks this named theorem directly as an actual-orbit nonrecurrence theorem.

---

Do **not** move fully to \(\mathbf M\) yet, but stop broad cell decomposition. Use a split allocation:

\quad
1\text{ agent on singular/canonical audit.}
}
\]

Suggested allocation:

   Attack the named theorem through \(O^++O^-\) and fixed-\(m_0\) same-cell off-diagonal control.

   Focus only on \(|\alpha|Q\ll1\) and the bilinear complementary-product condition \(nn'\approx m_0/(2\pi)\).

   Start deriving
   \[

   \]

4. **Agent 4 — \(\mathbf M\) subset-product resonance.**  
   Classify
   \[
   \Omega_S-\Omega_T=\log(N_S/N_T),
   \qquad |S|=|T|=R+1.
   \]

5. **Agent 5 — Direct \(\mathbf M\) lower-bound formulation.**  
   Formulate
   \[

   \]
   and compare pointwise versus averaged zero-set versions.

6. **Agent 6 — Canonical/singular audit.**

---

# 1. Status of the two cell subtheorems

## A. Same-Branch Cell Nonrecurrence

Target:

\[
|B^+|_2^2+|B^-|_2^2\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Equivalent off-diagonal form:

\[
O^+ + O^-
\ge
-(1-Q^{-A})(D^+ + D^-).
\]

Status:

\[
\boxed{
\text{not proved.}
}
\]

What is known:

\[
D^+,\ D^-
\]

are polynomially non-small after the intended normalization. Collapse requires almost complete same-cell off-diagonal cancellation:

\[
O^\pm\approx -D^\pm,
\]

equivalently

\[
B_r^\pm\approx0
\quad\forall r.
\]

This is false in phase-relaxed models and requires actual-orbit arithmetic:

\[
e^{-im_0\log n}.
\]

So this is now a named nonrecurrence input, not a local-algebra lemma.

---

## B. Folded Main/Dual Cell Nonalignment

Target:

\[
|\langle B^+,B^-\rangle|
\le
(1-Q^{-A})|B^+|_2|B^-|_2.

\ge
Q^{-A}(|B^+|_2+|B^-|_2).

}

branch dominance should close the main/dual nonalignment once same-branch mass is known.

\[

\]

the problem remains folded bilinear actual-orbit nonrecurrence, with the arithmetic relation

No sign, positivity, or purely local obstruction has been found.

=

Move to:

\Rightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}.

The natural averaged formulation is the zero-set large sieve:

\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).

=

+
\text{dual/background/error},

with

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S,
\qquad
|S|=R+1.
\]

The main technical object is the zero-sampling kernel:

\[
K_J(\xi)
=
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi}.
\]

The target is to control

\[

\]

well enough that diagonal exterior coefficient mass cannot be canceled by subset-product off-diagonal terms.

## Agent 6 / F — Singular branch separation and next roadmap

The ordinary \(\mathbf M\)-route and the deep singular branch should remain separate. They share the broad “actual-zeta anti-design” theme, but they use different scales, different prime ranges, and different analytic objects. The uploaded coordinator prompt explicitly parks the Fixed-Width Cell Nonrecurrence Theorem as a named input and moves the ordinary route to the cofactor vector theorem for \(\mathbf M\). 

---

# 1. Ordinary-route sequence

The ordinary Gate 1 route is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Fixed-Width Cell Nonrecurrence}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\]

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\]

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\]

\[
\boxed{
|\langle \mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha),\mathbf M(m_0)\rangle|
\ge Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\]

\[
\boxed{
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite-jet anti-design}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Gate 1}.
}
\]

The next ordinary-route theorem is therefore:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Longrightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The preferred averaged formulation is:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\ Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
}
\]

with

\[
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_{|S|=R+1}
\mathbf A_S e^{-im\Omega_S}
+
\text{dual/background/error},
\]

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S,
\qquad
N_S=\prod_{n\in S}n.
\]

The core kernel is:

\[
\boxed{
K_J(\xi)
=
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\ Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi}.
}
\]

The hard estimates are for

\[
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T).
\]

---

# 2. Why the singular branch is separate

The deep singular branch is

\[
\boxed{
\lambda Q\ll(\log Q)^{-C}.
}
\]

In this regime, ordinary packet-frequency Wronskians are not appropriate because the normalized ordinary nodes collapse:

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n,
\qquad
\log n\asymp Q,
\]

so

\[
x_n\asymp\lambda Q\ll1.
\]

Thus the ordinary Wronskian rank mechanism degenerates.

Instead, the singular branch uses local scaling. Write

\[
m=\gamma+cx,
\qquad c=\lambda.
\]

Then

\[
q''_{\can}(\gamma+cx)
\sim
c^{-3}Q_{\rm sing}''(x),
\]

and

\[
\int_{I_\rho}|q''_{\can}(m)|^2\,dm
\sim
c^{-5}\int_X|Q_{\rm sing}''(x)|^2\,dx.
\]

Therefore Gate 1 is won by dominance unless

\[
\boxed{
\|Q_{\rm sing}''\|_{L^2(X)}<Q^{-A}.
}
\]

That exceptional case is the singular anti-design event:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_j w_j\sin(k\theta_j)+\mathcal B_k\approx0,
\qquad
3\le k\le R+3.
}
\]

The explicit formula sends this to a prime-vector target:

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\mathbf P_R-\mathbf G_R-\mathbf T_R
\right\|_2
\le Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The needed singular theorem is the exclusion:

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\mathbf P_R-\mathbf G_R-\mathbf T_R
\right\|_2
\ge Q^{-A'}.
}
\]

The relevant prime range is not the ordinary packet-frequency range \(\log n\asymp Q\). To detect \(R\) local sine moments with analytic/band-limited probes, one needs roughly

\[
\boxed{
\log n\lesssim \frac{R^C}{c}.
}
\]

Since

\[
cQ\ll(\log Q)^{-C},
\]

this can be much longer than \(Q\). Hence the singular theorem is a long-prime actual-vector nonrecurrence problem, not the same as the ordinary \(\mathbf M\) zero-set large sieve.

---

# 3. Can \(\mathbf M\)-large-sieve machinery transfer to the singular branch?

Only at a very abstract level.

Both problems have the form:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite-dimensional packet-mode vector cannot be too small.}
}
\]

But the concrete arithmetic differs.

## Ordinary \(\mathbf M\) branch

Frequency family:

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S,
\qquad
|S|=R+1,
\]

with

\[
\log n\asymp Q.
\]

Sampling set:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_j)=0.
\]

Kernel:

\[
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T).
\]

This is a zero-set large sieve for subset-product frequencies at the ordinary conductor scale.

## Singular branch

Test vectors:

\[
\mathcal P_k(\gamma,c)
=
2\Re
\sum_n
\frac{\Lambda(n)}{\sqrt n}
n^{-i\gamma}
\widehat h_k(c\log n).
\]

Prime range:

\[
\log n\lesssim R^C/c.
\]

No \(Z_\alpha\)-zero sampling kernel is central here. The target is instead avoidance of a fixed vector:

\[
\mathbf P_R\not\approx \mathbf G_R+\mathbf T_R.
\]

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{\(\mathbf M\)-large-sieve methods may inspire singular probes, but they do not transfer directly.}
}
\]

The singular branch should remain a separate lane.

---

# 4. Roadmap for the \(\mathbf M\) theorem branch

## If kernel estimates progress

If Agent 1 obtains useful bounds for

\[
K_J(\xi),
\]

especially decay or cancellation for nonzero \(\xi\), then the next step is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{combine kernel estimates with subset-product resonance counts.}
}
\]

Specifically, prove:

\[
K_J(0)\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2
\gg
\left|
\sum_{S\ne T}
\langle \mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
\right|
\]

up to polynomial loss.

Next allocation:

- 2 agents on \(K_J\) estimates and argument-principle bounds;
- 2 agents on subset-product spacing/multiplicity;
- 1 agent on exterior coefficient mass;
- 1 agent on canonical audit.

## If subset-product resonances block progress

If many pairs satisfy

\[
\Omega_S-\Omega_T\approx0,
\]

or if exact collisions have high multiplicity, then the large sieve must be modified.

Possible pivots:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prime-only or squarefree packet support}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{weighted exterior basis suppressing resonant subsets}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{projected/coarse \(\mathbf M\)-mass rather than raw exterior mass}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{diagonalize by frequency clusters}
}
\]

Next allocation:

- 3 agents on resonance-robust exterior basis;
- 2 agents on modified zero-set large sieve;
- 1 agent on canonical transfer.

## If averaged-to-pointwise fails

If the averaged theorem

\[
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}
\]

does not imply

\[
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

at a specific admissible zero, then one needs a bad-zero exclusion principle:

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ll Q^{-A}
\Rightarrow
\text{many nearby zeros also have small \(\mathbf M\),}
}
\]

or a direct pointwise theorem:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Next allocation:

- 2 agents on derivative propagation of small \(\mathbf M\);
- 2 agents on local zero density and zero spacing;
- 2 agents on direct pointwise \(\mathbf M\)-nonrecurrence.

---

and

or

}
\]

Statement:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
}
\]

with a separate pointwise form if needed:

}

## Agent 6 / F — Singular branch separation and \(\mathbf M\)-round roadmap

The ordinary \(\mathbf M\)-large-sieve route and the deep singular anti-design branch must remain separate. The current round’s ordinary target is

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf M(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A},
\]

preferably through a zero-set large sieve over \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros. The singular branch instead remains a long-prime anti-design problem for

\[
\|\mathbf P_R-\mathbf G_R-\mathbf T_R\|_2\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

The coordinator prompt explicitly moves the main work to \(\mathbf M\) while keeping the singular branch separate. 

---

# 1. Why ordinary \(\mathbf M\)-large sieve does not cover deep singular anti-design

The ordinary branch works at

\[
\lambda Q\asymp1.
\]

The \(\mathbf M\) cofactor vector has expansion

\[
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_{|S|=R+1}
\mathbf A_S e^{-im\Omega_S}
+
\cdots,
\]

where

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S,
\qquad
N_S=\prod_{n\in S}n.
\]

The relevant ordinary packet-frequency range is

\[
\log n\asymp Q.
\]

So \(\mathbf M\) uses subset-product frequencies formed from conductor-scale packet frequencies.

The deep singular branch is instead

\[
\lambda Q\ll(\log Q)^{-C}.
\]

There, ordinary packet-frequency nodes collapse:

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n\asymp \lambda Q\ll1.
\]

Thus ordinary Wronskian or ordinary \(\mathbf M\)-large-sieve geometry does not see stable rank. The singular branch is handled by dominance unless a local same-scale sine anti-design occurs:

\[
\sum_j w_j\sin(k\theta_j)+\mathcal B_k\approx0,
\qquad
3\le k\le R+3.
\]

Explicit-formula probes for these sine moments require prime statistics of the form

\[
\mathcal P_k(\gamma,c)
=
2\Re
\sum_n
\frac{\Lambda(n)}{\sqrt n}
n^{-i\gamma}
\widehat h_k(c\log n),
\]

with prime range roughly

\[
\log n\lesssim \frac{R^C}{c}.
\]

Since \(cQ\ll(\log Q)^{-C}\), this is generally much longer than the ordinary \(\log n\asymp Q\) range.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{ordinary \(\mathbf M\)-large sieve is a conductor-scale subset-product problem;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{deep singular anti-design is a long-prime vector nonrecurrence problem.}
}
\]

They are not the same theorem.

---

# 2. Frequency comparison

## Ordinary \(\mathbf M\) branch

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S=\sum_{n\in S}\log n,
\qquad
|S|=R+1,
\]

with

\[
\log n\asymp Q.
\]

Frequency differences are

\[
\Omega_S-\Omega_T
=
\log(N_S/N_T).
\]

The zero-set large sieve studies

\[
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T),
\]

where

\[
K_J(\xi)
=
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi}.
\]

This is a zero-correlation/sampling problem over \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros.

## Deep singular branch

The singular explicit-formula vector uses frequencies through

\[
\widehat h_k(c\log n),
\]

with effective support

\[
c\log n\lesssim R^C,
\]

hence

\[
\log n\lesssim R^C/c.
\]

This is not sampled over \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros in the same way. It asks whether the prime vector can hit a prescribed target:

\[
\mathbf P_R(\gamma,c)\approx \mathbf G_R+\mathbf T_R.
\]

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
K_J(\xi)\text{ technology may inspire singular estimates, but it does not transfer directly.}
}
\]

---

# 3. Does \(K_J(\xi)\) transfer to the singular branch?

Only abstractly.

The common template is:

\[
\text{finite-dimensional packet-mode vector cannot be too small.}
\]

But the ordinary route’s kernel

\[
K_J(\xi)
\]

is tied to sampling on the \(Z_\alpha\)-zero set. The singular branch’s prime vector

\[
\mathbf P_R(\gamma,c)
\]

is a pointwise explicit-formula statistic at a singular center \(\gamma\), using long-prime probes.

A direct transfer would require a new scale-uniform theorem controlling prime-vector anti-concentration across all \(s=\lambda\) scales:

\[
\left\|
\left(
2\Re\sum_n \frac{\Lambda(n)}{\sqrt n}n^{-im}\widehat\Psi_j(s\log n)
\right)_j
-\mathbf T
\right\|_2
\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

That theorem would be stronger than both current branches and would require long-prime control. It is not a reasonable dependency for the ordinary \(\mathbf M\)-round.

So the singular branch should remain parked as its own named input:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Singular Explicit-Formula Anti-Design Exclusion.}
}
\]

---

# 4. Stopping criteria for the \(\mathbf M\)-round

## Case A — Kernel theorem succeeds

If Agent 1 obtains a usable theorem of the form

\[
K_J(\xi)
=
\delta_{\xi=0}K_J(0)+\mathcal E_J(\xi)
\]

with an operator bound strong enough for

\[
\sum_{S,T}
\langle\mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
\mathcal E_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T),
\]

then continue the \(\mathbf M\)-round.

2\text{ agents on kernel estimates,}
\quad

\quad

\quad
1\text{ on canonical audit.}
}
\]

Goal:

\[
K_J(0)\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2
\gg
\left|
\sum_{S\ne T}
\langle\mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
\right|.
\]

---

## Case B — Subset-product resonances block progress

If Agent 2 finds that near-collisions

\[
|\Omega_S-\Omega_T|\le \eta
\]

are too dense, or exact collisions have large multiplicity, the raw exterior-frequency basis is not adequate.

Then pivot to resonance-management:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prime-only support, squarefree restrictions, or resonance-cluster diagonalization.}
}
\]

Next allocation:

\[
\boxed{
3\text{ agents on resonance-robust exterior bases,}
\quad

\quad
1\text{ on canonical transfer.}
}
\]

If even after restrictions resonances remain uncontrollable, name the input:

\[
